  <title>
   Train conductor assists in baby's delivery | The Poughkeepsie Journal | poughkeepsiejournal.com
  </title>
  <meta content="Train conductor assists in baby's delivery" property="og:title"/>
  <meta content="article" property="og:type"/>
  <meta content="Columnists,poughkeepsiejournal.com,Poughkeepsie,New York,News,local news,headlines,stories,world news" name="keywords"/>
  <meta content="Posters aboard trains and at Grand Central Terminal state 'Metro-North Delivers.' The promotional piece is part of a campaign designed to increase customer awareness of ticket-purchasing options online and by phone, with orders delivered by mail to a home or office.On July 6, Metro-North conductor Guy Scalfani gave new meaning to the poster's headline. During the 7:18 p.m. express run from Manhattan to Poughkeepsie, Scalfani's attentiveness and quick action resulted in the safe birth of a baby.'I noticed a young lady walking on the terminal platform with a little boy,' Scalfani said. 'She was pregnant and could hardly walk to the train.'As he made his rounds collecting tickets aboard the train, Scalfani kept an eye on the woman. 'I saw her standing up and asked how she was doing,' he said. 'She said that she was due to deliver a baby the next day. I decided to keep the compartment door open so I could watch her during the trip.'After observing the woman enter the train's restroom and remain there for an unusually long period, Scalfani ap-proached its door as it flew open. 'She told me that she could feel the baby's head,' he said. 'I immediately tried to calm her and get her into the vestibule area. She suddenly fell into my arms.'While tending to the woman, he called for medical assistance on the train's public address system. 'An EMT and a nurse aboard the train came rushing over,' he said. 'The woman was concerned about her little boy, but we assured her that a commuter was holding him and he was safe. In all honesty, everybody came and helped out.'Scalfani notified the rail traffic controller, who in turn arranged for police and an ambulance to meet the train at the Croton-Harmon station.'As the doors to the train opened, the baby was born,' Scalfani said. 'Once I was sure that everything was OK, I announced on the PA that Metro-North had a baby boy.  The entire trainload of passengers erupted into cheers.'Scalfani, a 17-year employee of Metro-North and a resident of Wallkill with two children of his own, said the experience was very rewarding.'When you see everything happening right in front of  you so fast, it is important to stay calm and communicate for assistance.'Scalfani said talk of the birth circulated for a number of days among customers. One regular commuter, who had rushed to the scene and offered a scarf for the newborn to be wrapped in, spoke with Scalfani days later.'I felt bad about her losing her scarf, but her only thought was of thanks that she had just had it cleaned,' he said. 'The commuters are wonderful.'The train's engineer, Gerry McGuire, described Scalfani as 'the type of person that will stop to help out whenever someone is in need.' Gratefully, his passengers are beneficiaries of that attribute." name="description"/>
  <meta content="Posters aboard trains and at Grand Central Terminal state 'Metro-North Delivers.' The promotional piece is part of a campaign designed to increase customer awareness of ticket-purchasing options online and by phone, with orders delivered by mail to a home or office.On July 6, Metro-North conductor Guy Scalfani gave new meaning to the poster's headline. During the 7:18 p.m. express run from Manhattan to Poughkeepsie, Scalfani's attentiveness and quick action resulted in the safe birth of a baby.'I noticed a young lady walking on the terminal platform with a little boy,' Scalfani said. 'She was pregnant and could hardly walk to the train.'As he made his rounds collecting tickets aboard the train, Scalfani kept an eye on the woman. 'I saw her standing up and asked how she was doing,' he said. 'She said that she was due to deliver a baby the next day. I decided to keep the compartment door open so I could watch her during the trip.'After observing the woman enter the train's restroom and remain there for an unusually long period, Scalfani ap-proached its door as it flew open. 'She told me that she could feel the baby's head,' he said. 'I immediately tried to calm her and get her into the vestibule area. She suddenly fell into my arms.'While tending to the woman, he called for medical assistance on the train's public address system. 'An EMT and a nurse aboard the train came rushing over,' he said. 'The woman was concerned about her little boy, but we assured her that a commuter was holding him and he was safe. In all honesty, everybody came and helped out.'Scalfani notified the rail traffic controller, who in turn arranged for police and an ambulance to meet the train at the Croton-Harmon station.'As the doors to the train opened, the baby was born,' Scalfani said. 'Once I was sure that everything was OK, I announced on the PA that Metro-North had a baby boy.  The entire trainload of passengers erupted into cheers.'Scalfani, a 17-year employee of Metro-North and a resident of Wallkill with two children of his own, said the experience was very rewarding.'When you see everything happening right in front of  you so fast, it is important to stay calm and communicate for assistance.'Scalfani said talk of the birth circulated for a number of days among customers. One regular commuter, who had rushed to the scene and offered a scarf for the newborn to be wrapped in, spoke with Scalfani days later.'I felt bad about her losing her scarf, but her only thought was of thanks that she had just had it cleaned,' he said. 'The commuters are wonderful.'The train's engineer, Gerry McGuire, described Scalfani as 'the type of person that will stop to help out whenever someone is in need.' Gratefully, his passengers are beneficiaries of that attribute." property="og:description"/>
